Prohibition of Covering Face in Ihram
Prohibition of covering head and face, with dispute on face covering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi prohibition on covering the face during ihram and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
A muhrim's face must remain uncovered; the Prophet's command regarding the deceased Bedouin proves it.

This passage addresses a key point of ikhtilaf between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: may a male muhrim cover his face? The Hanafi school holds it is prohibited, citing a hadith where the Prophet forbade covering the face of a deceased muhrim. The Shafi'i school relies on a different hadith differentiating ihram of man and woman. We will examine the proofs and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Recognize that covering the head and face with anything that normally covers is prohibited for men. The ruling is that it is haram.
Women cover their heads but not their faces.
2
State Hanafi Position and Proof
The Hanafi position: covering the face is prohibited. The proof is the hadith of the Bedouin muhrim: 'Do not cover his face nor his head.'
This hadith is marfu' and directly forbids covering.
3
State Shafi'i Position and Proof
Imam al-Shafi'i permits covering the face based on the hadith: 'The ihram of a man is in his head, and the ihram of a woman is in her face.'
This hadith is mawquf on Ibn Umar.
4
Analyze Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response distinguishes the case of a deceased muhrim (where covering is allowed because ihram ends with death) and interprets the Shafi'i hadith as not explicitly permitting face covering.
The Bedouin hadith is stronger because it is marfu' and directly negates covering.
5
Determine the Thamarah
The practical consequence: Hanafi school requires a dam if a man covers his face; Shafi'i school permits it without penalty.
This ikhtilaf affects actual practice during Hajj.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وستر الرأس والوجه وغسلهما بالخطمي، أي واتق أيضاً وستر الرأس والوجه بما يغطى به عادة بخلاف نحو العدل والطبق، وهذا مختص بالرجال، أما المرأة فتستر رأسها لا وجهها. (ط) ولو حمل على رأسه ثياباً كان تغطية لا حمل عدل وطبق ما لم يمتد يوما وليلة فتلزمه صدقة، وقالوا: لو دخل تحت ستر الكعبة فأصاب رأسه أو وجهه كره وإلا فلا بأس به، وكذا لو غطت المرأة ولم تجاف عن وجهها؛ لأن تغطية الوجه حرام عليها كالرجل، ولو غطى رجل رأس محرم نائم يوما لزمه دم؛ لأن الستر حرام لما فيه من معنى الارتفاق، وهذا حاصل بفعل الغير، وقال الشافعي: يجوز للرجال تغطية وجهه؛ لقوله عليه السلام: "إحرام الرجل في رأسه وإحرام المرأة في وجهها" ولنا قوله عليه السلام في الأعرابي المحرم الذي وقصت به ناقته فمات "لا تخمروا وجهه ولا رأسه فإنه يبعث يوم القيامة ملبيا"، فنهيه تخمير وجهه ورأسه دل على أن للإحرام أثرا في عدم تغطية الوجه غير أن أصحابنا قالوا بتغطية وجه المحرم إذا مات بدليل آخر، وهو ما روي: أنه عليه السلام سئل عن محرم مات فأمر بتخمير وجهه ورأسه، وإنما أمر بذلك لانقطاع الإحرام بالموت؛ لقوله عليه السلام: "إذا مات ابن آدم انقطع عمله" الحديث، ولا شك أن الإحرام عمل، وتأويل حديث الأعرابي أن النبي عليه السلام عرف بقاء إحرامه بعد الموت بطريق الوحي بالخصوصية، وقد كان النبي عليه السلام يخص بعض أصحابه بأشياء، ولهذا نهاهم عن تخميرهما، وأيضاً ما رواه الشافعي موقوف على ابن عمر لهما فلا يعارض المرفوع، ولئن صح فقوله: "إحرام الرجل في رأسه" ليس فيه نفي وجهه. (عيني، فتح، مستخلص) وغسلهما أي الرأس والوجه أراد بالوجه اللحية من إطلاق المحل وإرادة الحال بقرينة قوله: "بالخطمي"؛ لأن الوجه لا يغسل به عادة، وإنما منع؛ لأنه طيب عند الإمام، وله رائحة طيبة فصار كاستعمال الطيب، وعندهما: يلين الشعر ويحسنه ويقتل القمل والثمرة تظهر في استعماله فعند الإمام يلزمه دم وعندهما: صدقة، بخلاف الصابون والأشنان حيث لا يلزمه باستعماله شيء اتفاقاً. (عيني، فتح)
And covering the head and face and washing them with khitmi. Meaning: beware also of covering the head and face with what normally covers, unlike the load and the tray, and this is specific to men; as for the woman, she covers her head but not her face. (T.) And if he carries clothes on his head, it is covering, not carrying a load or tray, unless it continues for a day and a night, then sadaqah is incumbent upon him. And they said: If he enters under the covering of the Kaaba and touches his head or face, it is disliked, otherwise there is no harm. Similarly, if a woman covers [her face] and does not leave it open, because covering the face is prohibited for her like the man. And if a man covers the head of a sleeping muhrim for a day, a dam is incumbent upon him, because covering is prohibited due to the meaning of benefit, and this occurs by the act of another. And al-Shafi'i said: It is permissible for men to cover their faces, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The ihram of a man is in his head, and the ihram of a woman is in her face.' And we have his saying, peace be upon him, regarding the Bedouin muhrim whose camel threw him and he died: 'Do not cover his face nor his head, for he will be raised on the Day of Resurrection saying labbayk.' So his prohibition of covering his face and head indicates that ihram has an effect regarding not covering the face. However, our companions said about covering the face of a muhrim when he dies, due to another evidence, which is that it was narrated that he, peace be upon him, was asked about a muhrim who died, and he ordered that his face and head be covered. He ordered that only due to the cessation of ihram upon death, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'When the son of Adam dies, his deeds are cut off' – the hadith. And there is no doubt that ihram is a deed. And the interpretation of the hadith of the Bedouin is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, knew the continuity of his ihram after death by way of revelation through specific knowledge. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, used to single out some of his companions with things, and because of that he forbade them from covering his face and head. Also, what al-Shafi'i narrated as a mawquf hadith from Ibn Umar is not contradictory to the marfu' hadith, and even if authentic, his saying 'The ihram of a man is in his head' does not negate his face. (Ayni, Fath, Mustakhlas) And washing them, i.e., the head and face; he meant by the face the beard, by way of using the place to mean the thing that occupies it, indicated by his saying 'with khitmi', because the face is not usually washed with it. It is prohibited because, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is a perfume, having a pleasant smell, so it becomes like using perfume. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): it softens the hair, improves it, and kills lice, and the benefit appears in its use, so according to Imam, a dam is obligatory, and according to them, sadaqah. Unlike soap and ash, where nothing is obligatory by using them by agreement. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage deals with two prohibitions: covering the head/face and washing them with khitmi (a type of plant used for washing). The commentary explains that covering the head and face with anything that normally covers is prohibited for men; women must cover their head but not their face. It then discusses cases of partially covering or covering by another person. An inter-madhhab dispute arises: Imam al-Shafi'i permits men to cover their faces based on the hadith 'Ihram of a man is in his head, and ihram of a woman is in her face.' The Hanafi response references the hadith about the Bedouin muhrim who died, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade covering his face and head, indicating the prohibition. They also distinguish the case of a deceased muhrim: our scholars (ashabuna) say that if a muhrim dies, his face and head should be covered because ihram ends with death. The second part addresses washing the head and face with khitmi. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, khitmi is considered a perfume because it has a pleasant scent, so using it requires a dam (sacrifice). According to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it has benefits like softening hair and killing lice, so only sadaqah is due. Soap and ash are permitted without penalty. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
ستر (satr): Covering; the act of covering the head or face.
المحرم (al-muhrim): A person in the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah.
الوجه (al-wajh): Face; the part of the body that the Hanafi school rules must remain uncovered for men in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a male muhrim covering his face during ihram?
A) Permissible, no expiation
B) Permissible, but disliked
C) Prohibited, requires dam
D) Prohibited, invalidates ihram

Key Takeaway
Covering the face is prohibited for male muhrims, requiring a dam if violated, and this is upheld by a strong marfu' hadith.

Washing with Khitmi During Ihram
Prohibition of washing head and face with khitmi, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on using khitmi during ihram and apply the correct ruling regarding expiation.

Core Idea
Khitmi is considered perfume by Imam Abu Hanifa, requiring a dam; the Sahibayn consider it beneficial only, requiring sadaqah.

This passage also discusses washing the head and beard with khitmi (marsh mallow) during ihram. Imam Abu Hanifa views it as a perfume due to its pleasant scent, making its use prohibited and requiring a dam. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it a useful cleanser and require only sadaqah. This intra-Hanafi difference is based on the underlying cause (illa) of the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Rulng
The act is washing the head and beard with khitmi. The base ruling is that it is prohibited because it is considered a perfume (according to Imam).
The prohibition is not absolute; the expiation differs.
2
State Imam's Position and Basis
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that using khitmi requires a dam because it has a pleasant scent, thus it is analogous to perfume (qiyas).
The scent is the effective cause (illa).
3
State Sahibayn's Position and Basis
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that only sadaqah is required because khitmi has benefits (softens hair, kills lice) and its scent is not the primary purpose.
They focus on the benefit rather than the scent.
4
Analyze the Qiyas
Imam's qiyas: Asl is perfume (haram, dam). Far' is khitmi. Illa is pleasant scent. Sahibayn object that the illa is not present in khitmi because it is primarily a cleanser.
The disagreement is on the existence of the illa.
5
Determine the Thamarah
Practical difference: if a pilgrim uses khitmi, according to Imam he owes a dam; according to Sahibayn, only sadaqah.
This affects the amount of expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ط) ولو حمل على رأسه ثياباً كان تغطية لا حمل عدل وطبق ما لم يمتد يوما وليلة فتلزمه صدقة، وقالوا: لو دخل تحت ستر الكعبة فأصاب رأسه أو وجهه كره وإلا فلا بأس به، وكذا لو غطت المرأة ولم تجاف عن وجهها؛ لأن تغطية الوجه حرام عليها كالرجل، ولو غطى رجل رأس محرم نائم يوما لزمه دم؛ لأن الستر حرام لما فيه من معنى الارتفاق، وهذا حاصل بفعل الغير، وقال الشافعي: يجوز للرجال تغطية وجهه؛ لقوله عليه السلام: "إحرام الرجل في رأسه وإحرام المرأة في وجهها" ولنا قوله عليه السلام في الأعرابي المحرم الذي وقصت به ناقته فمات "لا تخمروا وجهه ولا رأسه فإنه يبعث يوم القيامة ملبيا"، فنهيه تخمير وجهه ورأسه دل على أن للإحرام أثرا في عدم تغطية الوجه غير أن أصحابنا قالوا بتغطية وجه المحرم إذا مات بدليل آخر، وهو ما روي: أنه عليه السلام سئل عن محرم مات فأمر بتخمير وجهه ورأسه، وإنما أمر بذلك لانقطاع الإحرام بالموت؛ لقوله عليه السلام: "إذا مات ابن آدم انقطع عمله" الحديث، ولا شك أن الإحرام عمل، وتأويل حديث الأعرابي أن النبي عليه السلام عرف بقاء إحرامه بعد الموت بطريق الوحي بالخصوصية، وقد كان النبي عليه السلام يخص بعض أصحابه بأشياء، ولهذا نهاهم عن تخميرهما، وأيضاً ما رواه الشافعي موقوف على ابن عمر لهما فلا يعارض المرفوع، ولئن صح فقوله: "إحرام الرجل في رأسه" ليس فيه نفي وجهه. (عيني، فتح، مستخلص) وغسلهما أي الرأس والوجه أراد بالوجه اللحية من إطلاق المحل وإرادة الحال بقرينة قوله: "بالخطمي"؛ لأن الوجه لا يغسل به عادة، وإنما منع؛ لأنه طيب عند الإمام، وله رائحة طيبة فصار كاستعمال الطيب، وعندهما: يلين الشعر ويحسنه ويقتل القمل والثمرة تظهر في استعماله فعند الإمام يلزمه دم وعندهما: صدقة، بخلاف الصابون والأشنان حيث لا يلزمه باستعماله شيء اتفاقاً. (عيني، فتح)
And covering the head and face and washing them with khitmi. Meaning: beware also of covering the head and face with what normally covers, unlike the load and the tray, and this is specific to men; as for the woman, she covers her head but not her face. (T.) And if he carries clothes on his head, it is covering, not carrying a load or tray, unless it continues for a day and a night, then sadaqah is incumbent upon him. And they said: If he enters under the covering of the Kaaba and touches his head or face, it is disliked, otherwise there is no harm. Similarly, if a woman covers [her face] and does not leave it open, because covering the face is prohibited for her like the man. And if a man covers the head of a sleeping muhrim for a day, a dam is incumbent upon him, because covering is prohibited due to the meaning of benefit, and this occurs by the act of another. And al-Shafi'i said: It is permissible for men to cover their faces, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The ihram of a man is in his head, and the ihram of a woman is in her face.' And we have his saying, peace be upon him, regarding the Bedouin muhrim whose camel threw him and he died: 'Do not cover his face nor his head, for he will be raised on the Day of Resurrection saying labbayk.' So his prohibition of covering his face and head indicates that ihram has an effect regarding not covering the face. However, our companions said about covering the face of a muhrim when he dies, due to another evidence, which is that it was narrated that he, peace be upon him, was asked about a muhrim who died, and he ordered that his face and head be covered. He ordered that only due to the cessation of ihram upon death, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'When the son of Adam dies, his deeds are cut off' – the hadith. And there is no doubt that ihram is a deed. And the interpretation of the hadith of the Bedouin is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, knew the continuity of his ihram after death by way of revelation through specific knowledge. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, used to single out some of his companions with things, and because of that he forbade them from covering his face and head. Also, what al-Shafi'i narrated as a mawquf hadith from Ibn Umar is not contradictory to the marfu' hadith, and even if authentic, his saying 'The ihram of a man is in his head' does not negate his face. (Ayni, Fath, Mustakhlas) And washing them, i.e., the head and face; he meant by the face the beard, by way of using the place to mean the thing that occupies it, indicated by his saying 'with khitmi', because the face is not usually washed with it. It is prohibited because, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is a perfume, having a pleasant smell, so it becomes like using perfume. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): it softens the hair, improves it, and kills lice, and the benefit appears in its use, so according to Imam, a dam is obligatory, and according to them, sadaqah. Unlike soap and ash, where nothing is obligatory by using them by agreement. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage deals with two prohibitions: covering the head/face and washing them with khitmi (a type of plant used for washing). The commentary explains that covering the head and face with anything that normally covers is prohibited for men; women must cover their head but not their face. It then discusses cases of partially covering or covering by another person. An inter-madhhab dispute arises: Imam al-Shafi'i permits men to cover their faces based on the hadith 'Ihram of a man is in his head, and ihram of a woman is in her face.' The Hanafi response references the hadith about the Bedouin muhrim who died, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade covering his face and head, indicating the prohibition. They also distinguish the case of a deceased muhrim: our scholars (ashabuna) say that if a muhrim dies, his face and head should be covered because ihram ends with death. The second part addresses washing the head and face with khitmi. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, khitmi is considered a perfume because it has a pleasant scent, so using it requires a dam (sacrifice). According to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it has benefits like softening hair and killing lice, so only sadaqah is due. Soap and ash are permitted without penalty. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
الخطمي (al-khitmī): A plant (marsh mallow) used for washing; considered a perfume by Imam Abu Hanifa due to its scent.
الدم (ad-dam): Sacrificial animal (a sheep) required as expiation for certain ihram violations.
الصدقة (as-sadaqah): Charity; a lesser expiation, often half a sa' of wheat or equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Dam (sacrifice)

Key Takeaway
Using scented plants like khitmi is prohibited in ihram, with a difference on expiation: dam (Imam) or sadaqah (Sahibayn).

Perfume and Henna During Ihram: Prohibition and Dispute
Prohibits touching perfume and addresses the henna dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis prohibit henna as a perfume in ihram and distinguish the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Henna is a perfume in Hanafi law, so applying it in ihram violates the state of ihram.

This passage addresses two related rules: the general prohibition of using perfume during ihram and the specific dispute over henna. The Prophet described the pilgrim as 'disheveled and unkempt,' meaning free from perfume and beautification. However, Imam al-Shafi'i allowed henna because he did not consider it a perfume. The Hanafi school counters with a clear hadith: 'Henna is a perfume.' Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to evaluate textual evidence in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general ruling
Identify the act (touching/perfuming) and the evidence: the Prophet's statement 'al-hajj al-sha'ath al-tafil' and the associated hadith about Arafah. This establishes that the pilgrim must avoid perfumes entirely.
2
Isolate the disputed sub-ruling
Focus on henna (khidab). Al-Shafi'i permits it because he classifies it as a dye, not a perfume. State his position fairly.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response cites the Prophet's statement 'al-hinna' tib' (Henna is a perfume), narrated by al-Nasa'i. This explicit text overrides the general definition of henna as a dye.
The hadith is classified as hasan by some scholars; Hanafis accept it as sufficient evidence.
4
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'tib' (perfume) is a predicate (khabar) that attributes the quality of perfume to henna. In Hanafi usul, such direct attribution is binding and classifies henna under the prohibition of perfume in ihram.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The dispute affects the ruling on applying henna: Hanafis require a dam (penalty) for using it while in ihram; Shafi'is allow it without penalty. This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومس الطيب بقوله عليه السلام: "الحاج الشعث التفل"، والمراد من الشعث أن يكون منتشرا شعره ومغبراً، والمراد من التفل أن يترك الطيب حتى يوجد منه رائحة كريهة؛ لأنه يشبه الميت من حيث أنه انقطع عن وطنه، وقد قال عليه السلام: "إن الله تعالى يباهي على ملائكته يوم عرفة، ويقول: عبادي جاؤوا شعثا غبرا من كل فج عميق، يبتغون مرضاتي، ويلتمسون مغفرتي اشهدوا أني قد غفرت لهم"، وقال الشافعي: يجوز له الخضاب بالحناء؛ لأنه ليس بطيب، ولنا قوله عليه السلام: "الحناء طيب"، رواه النسائي. (عيني، مستخلص)
And touching perfume, by his saying, peace be upon him: 'The pilgrim is disheveled and unkempt.' The meaning of 'shath' is that his hair is disheveled and dusty, and the meaning of 'tafil' is that he abandons perfume until a foul smell emanates from him; because he resembles the dead in that he is cut off from his homeland. And he, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed Allah, the Exalted, boasts to His angels on the Day of Arafah and says: My servants have come disheveled and dusty from every deep valley, seeking My pleasure and asking for My forgiveness; bear witness that I have forgiven them.' And al-Shafi'i said: It is permissible for him to dye with henna, because it is not a perfume. And we have his saying, peace be upon him: 'Henna is a perfume,' narrated by al-Nasa'i. (Ayni, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage rules on the prohibition of using perfume during ihram. The proof is the Prophet's description of the pilgrim as 'al-shath al-tafil' – disheveled and not using perfume, implying that using perfume negates the state of ihram. The commentary also mentions that the pilgrim should appear like one cut off from worldly comforts, similar to a dead person. A supporting hadith states that Allah boasts about the pilgrims on Arafah who come disheveled and dusty. The text then presents an inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i permits dyeing with henna because he does not consider it a perfume. The Hanafi response cites the Prophet's statement 'Henna is a perfume' narrated by al-Nasa'i, thus prohibiting it. The Hanafi position is that henna is a perfume and therefore prohibited in ihram.

Key Terms
الحناء (al-ḥinnā'): Henna, a plant used for dyeing; considered a perfume in the Hanafi school.
الشعث التفل (ash-shaʿth at-tafil): Disheveled hair and not using perfume; the natural state of a pilgrim.

Prohibition of Covering Head and Face in Ihram and the Definition of Scented Garments
States prohibitions of certain clothing and covering head/face with proofs and Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi prohibition on covering the head and face for men in ihram, explain the criterion for prohibited dyed garments, and evaluate the Hanafi rebuttal to the Shafi'i argument.

Core Idea
A muhrim man must not cover his head or face; a garment that emits scent, even without transferring dye, is prohibited.

In ihram, restrictions go beyond the well-known prohibitions of hunting and conjugal relations. This lesson focuses on two subtle but critical prohibitions: wearing garments that have been dyed with scented substances, and covering the head or face. We will unpack the Hanafi definition of what makes a dyed garment prohibited, and then dive into an important inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i on whether a man may cover his face. By the end, you will understand the evidence and how the Hanafi school arrives at its position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on covering head and face
For men, covering the head or face is haram; for women, only face covering is haram.
2
Identify the opponent's view
Al-Shafi'i permits men to cover their face.
3
Examine the opponent's proof
The hadith 'Ihram of a man is in his head, and of a woman in her face' (mawquf on Ibn Umar).
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The explicit prohibition in the hadith of the deceased muhrim ('Do not cover his face nor his head') takes precedence. The Shafi'i narration is mawquf and does not explicitly permit.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Hanafi: covering face requires dam/sadaqah; Shafi'i: no penalty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محظورات الإحرام
إلا أن يكون غسيلاً لا ينفض وستر الرأس والوجه وغسلهما بالخطمي، ومس الطيب،
أي واتق أيضاً
أي واتق أيضاً
أن يكون غسيلاً: أي الثوب المصبوغ بأحد هذه الأشياء. (ط) لا ينفض [ أي لا يفوح، وقيل: لا يتناثر وهو أقرب لمادة اللفظ (عيني) بضم الفاء، والنفض تناثر الصبغ عند الفقهاء والتعويل على الرائحة، حتى لو كان لا يتناثر لكن يفوح ريحه يمنع منه المحرم كالثوب المبخر، وقيل: النفض: فوحان الطيب، وهو الأصح، وعند محمد: أحد الأمرين إما أن يتعدى أثر الصبغ إلى غيره وإن لم يفح، أو يفوح وإن لم يتعد أثر الصبغ إلى غيره. (فتح) وستر الرأس والوجه بما يغطى به عادة بخلاف نحو العدل والطبق، وهذا مختص بالرجال، أما المرأة فتستر رأسها لا وجهها. (ط)] ولو حمل على رأسه ثياباً كان تغطية لا حمل عدل وطبق ما لم يمتد يوما وليلة فتلزمه صدقة، وقالوا: لو دخل تحت ستر الكعبة فأصاب رأسه أو وجهه ،كره وإلا فلا بأس به، وكذا لو غطت المرأة ولم تجاف عن وجهها؛ لأن تغطية الوجه حرام عليها كالرجل، ولو غطى رجل رأس محرم نائم يوما لزمه دم؛ لأن الستر حرام لما فيه من معنى الارتفاق، وهذا حاصل بفعل الغير، وقال الشافعي: يجوز للرجال تغطية وجهه؛ لقوله عليها: "إحرام الرجل في رأسه وإحرام المرأة في وجهها ولنا قوله عليها في الأعرابي المحرم الذي وقصت به ناقته فمات "لا تخمروا وجهه ولا رأسه فإنه يبعث يوم القيامة ملبيا"، فنهيه تخمير وجهه ورأسه دل على أن للإحرام أثرا في عدم تغطية الوجه غير أن أصحابنا قالوا بتغطية وجه المحرم إذا مات بدليل آخر، وهو ما روي: أنه عليه سئل عن محرم مات فأمر بتخمير وجهه ورأسه، وإنما أمر بذلك لانقطاع الإحرام بالموت؛ لقوله عليه: "إذا مات ابن آدم انقطع عمله الحديث، ولا شك أن الإحرام عمل، وتأويل حديث الأعرابي أن النبي عليها عرف بقاء إحرامه بعد الموت بطريق الوحي بالخصوصية، وقد كان النبي عليه يخص بعض أصحابه بأشياء، ولهذا نهاهم عن تخميرهما، وأيضاً ما رواه الشافعي موقوف على ابن عمر لهما فلا يعارض المرفوع، ولإن صح فقوله: "إحرام الرجل في رأسه" ليس فيه نفي وجهه. (عيني، فتح، مستخلص)
Prohibitions of Ihram: Except if it is a garment dyed with something that does not shed, and covering the head and the face, and washing them with khithmi, and touching perfume, i.e., also abstain. 'That it is dyed': meaning a garment dyed with one of these things. (T) 'does not shed': i.e., does not emit fragrance, or it is said: does not scatter, which is closer to the literal meaning. (Ayni) The shedding is the scattering of dye according to jurists, but reliance is on scent, such that even if it does not scatter but its scent spreads, the muhrim is prevented from it, like a fumigated garment. It is said: shedding is the emission of perfume, which is the most correct. According to Muhammad, one of two matters: either the effect of the dye transfers to something else even if it does not emit, or it emits even if the dye does not transfer. (Fath) And covering the head and face with what is normally used for covering, unlike a load or a tray. This is specific to men; as for women, she covers her head, not her face. (T) If he carries clothes on his head, it is covering, not carrying a load or tray, unless it continues for a day and a night, then sadaqah is due. They said: if he enters under the covering of the Kaaba and it touches his head or face, it is disliked; otherwise, no harm. Similarly, if a woman covers without averting her face, because covering the face is prohibited for her just as for the man. If a man covers the head of a sleeping muhrim for a day, a dam is due, because covering is prohibited due to the meaning of benefit, and this is achieved by another's action. Al-Shafi'i said: It is permissible for men to cover their faces, based on his saying: 'The ihram of a man is in his head, and the ihram of a woman is in her face.' Our evidence is his saying regarding the Bedouin muhrim whose camel broke his neck and he died: 'Do not cover his face nor his head, for he will be raised on the Day of Judgment in talbiyah.' His prohibition of covering his face and head indicates that ihram has an effect on not covering the face. However, our companions said to cover the face of a deceased muhrim due to another evidence: it is narrated that he was asked about a muhrim who died, and he ordered covering his face and head. He ordered that only because ihram ends with death, due to his saying: 'When the son of Adam dies, his deeds cease.' There is no doubt that ihram is a deed. The interpretation of the Bedouin hadith is that the Prophet knew the continuation of his ihram after death by way of revelation specifically, and the Prophet used to single out some of his companions with things, hence he prohibited them from covering him. Also, what al-Shafi'i narrated as mawquf upon Ibn 'Umar does not counter the marfu'. If it is authentic, his saying 'the ihram of a man is in his head' does not negate his face. (Ayni, Fath, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage from the Muharramat al-Ihram section details two key prohibitions: wearing garments dyed with scented substances, and covering the head/face. It opens by defining 'la yunfad' – a garment that does not shed dye or emit scent. The Hanafi position relies on scent: if the garment gives off fragrance, it is prohibited even if no dye transfers. Muhammad al-Shaybani has a broader view: prohibition applies if either dye transfers or scent spreads. The passage then addresses covering the head and face. For men, covering either is absolutely prohibited; for women, they cover their head but must leave their face uncovered. The text provides specific scenarios: carrying clothes on the head is considered covering (not merely carrying a load), and if it continues a full day, sadaqah is due. Entering under the Kaaba's covering is disliked only if it touches the head/face. A man covering a sleeping muhrim's head for a day requires a dam, as the prohibition of covering applies even when done by another. The passage then presents a major inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who permits men to cover their face in ihram, citing the hadith 'Ihram of a man is in his head, and of a woman in her face.' The Hanafi response counters with the hadith of the deceased muhrim: the Prophet explicitly ordered not to cover his face or head, proving that covering is prohibited during life. The apparent contradiction regarding covering a deceased muhrim is resolved: ihram ends with death, so covering becomes permissible then. The Shafi'i narration from Ibn Umar is dismissed as mawquf (non-prophetic) and thus weaker. Thus, the Hanafi position stands: covering the head or face of a living muhrim is prohibited, and the penalty for violation is a dam or sadaqah depending on duration and method.

Key Terms
غسيل (ghusayl): A garment dyed with one of the prohibited scented substances.
ينفض (yanfud): Shedding of dye or emission of fragrance; the criterion for prohibition of wearing such a garment.
ستر (satr): Covering; specifically of the head and face in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a male muhrim covers his head with a cloth for most of the day (from sunrise to sunset). What compensation is required?
A) No compensation, as he may not have intended to cover
B) Sadaqah, because it is less than a full day and night
C) Dam, because the covering lasted a full day
D) The ihram becomes invalid

Key Takeaway
A muhrim man must avoid covering his head and face; the Hanafi school deems this haram with compensation, supported by explicit prophetic command.

Washing Head/Face with Khithmi and Touching Perfume in Ihram
Details the prohibition of washing head/face with khithmi and touching perfume, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and an inter-madhhab dispute on henna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using khithmi in ihram, explain the intra-Hanafi difference on the required compensation, and apply the hadith 'Henna is a perfume' to evaluate the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
In ihram, even a scented wash like khithmi is prohibited, but the penalty differs among the Hanafi imams.

This lesson examines two prohibited acts in ihram: washing the head or face with khithmi and touching perfume in general. The passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the ruling, notes an intra-Hanafi difference regarding the penalty for using khithmi, and then addresses a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over the use of henna. By understanding the reasoning behind these rulings, you will appreciate how the Hanafi school derives rules from textual evidence and practical considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and the ruling
The text rules that using khithmi on the head/face is prohibited, and touching any perfume is prohibited in ihram.
Khithmi is a scented plant; its pleasant scent makes it akin to perfume.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on khithmi
Imam Abu Hanifa considers khithmi a pure perfume requiring a dam (sacrifice); Abu Yusuf and Muhammad view it as a beneficial wash requiring only sadaqah (charity).
The difference stems from whether the scent or the cleansing benefit predominates.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is the amount of compensation: dam is a major penalty (one sheep), sadaqah is minor (half sa' of wheat).
Both views agree on the prohibition.
4
Examine the inter-madhhab dispute on henna
Shafi'i permits henna dye as it is not perfume; Hanafi prohibits it based on the hadith 'Henna is a perfume'.
The Hanafi position relies on explicit prophetic text.
5
Apply the rule to practice
A pilgrim must avoid any scented substance on the head/face; if used, the stricter compensation (dam) is often preferred out of caution.
Unscented soap/ash is allowed by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغسلهما أي الرأس والوجه أراد بالوجه اللحية من إطلاق المحل وإرادة الحال بقرينة قوله: "بالخطمي"؛ لأن الوجه لا يغسل به عادة، وإنما منع؛ لأنه طيب عند الإمام، وله رائحة طيبة فصار كاستعمال الطيب، وعندهما: يلين الشعر ويحسنه ويقتل القمل والثمرة تظهر في استعماله فعند الإمام يلزمه دم وعندهما: صدقة، بخلاف الصابون والأشنان حيث لا يلزمه باستعماله شيء اتفاقاً. (عيني، فتح) ومس الطيب بقوله علي: "الحاج الشعث التفل"، والمراد من الشعث أن يكون منتشرا شعره ومغبراً، والمراد من التفل أن يترك الطيب حتى يوجد منه رائحة كريهة؛ لأنه يشبه الميت من حيث أنه انقطع عن وطنه، وقد قال علي: "إن الله تعالى يباهي على ملائكته يوم عرفة، ويقول: "عبادي جاؤوا شعثاء غبراء من كل فج عميق، يبتغون مرضاتي، ويلتمسون مغفرتي اشهدوا أني قد غفرت لهم، وقال الشافعي: يجوز له الخضاب بالحناء؛ لأنه ليس بطيب، ولنا قوله علية: الحناء طيب"، رواه النسائي. (عيني، مستخلص)
And washing them, i.e., the head and the face. By 'face' he means the beard – a metonymy of place for occupant, as indicated by his saying 'with khithmi' because the face is not normally washed with it. It is prohibited because according to Imam Abu Hanifa it is a perfume, having a pleasant scent, so it becomes like using perfume. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it softens the hair, improves it, and kills lice, and the benefit appears in its use. So according to Imam: a dam is due; according to them: sadaqah. Unlike soap and ash (ushnan) where no compensation is required by consensus. (Ayni, Fath) And touching perfume based on his saying: 'The pilgrim is unkempt and unperfumed.' The meaning of 'unkempt' is that his hair is disheveled and dusty. The meaning of 'unperfumed' is that he leaves perfume until a foul smell emanates from him, because he resembles a dead person as he is cut off from his homeland. And he said: 'Indeed Allah boasts to His angels on the Day of Arafah and says: My servants have come disheveled and dusty from every deep valley, seeking My pleasure and seeking My forgiveness; bear witness that I have forgiven them.' Al-Shafi'i said: It is permissible for him to dye with henna because it is not perfume. Our evidence is his statement: 'Henna is a perfume,' narrated by Nasa'i. (Ayni, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage continues the Muharramat al-Ihram, focusing on two acts: washing the head/face with khithmi (a scented plant) and touching perfume. The text explains that 'washing the face' actually refers to the beard, as the face is not normally washed with khithmi. Khithmi is prohibited because its pleasant scent makes it akin to perfume. However, there is an intra-Hanafi difference: Imam Abu Hanifa considers it a pure scent (tib) and requires a dam (major compensation) for its use. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) view it as having additional benefits (softening hair, killing lice) and thus only require sadaqah (minor compensation). Both positions agree on prohibition, differing only on the penalty. Simple soap and ash (ushnan) incur no penalty by consensus. The second part addresses touching perfume in general. The hadith 'the pilgrim is unkempt and unperfumed' establishes that the pilgrim should avoid perfume until a natural foul smell develops, symbolizing detachment from worldly comforts. The text cites a hadith qudsi where Allah boasts about the disheveled pilgrims. The passage then presents a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who permits dyeing with henna on the grounds that it is not perfume. The Hanafi response is direct: the Prophet explicitly said 'Henna is a perfume' (as recorded by Nasa'i), thus its use is prohibited like any other perfume. This ruling applies uniformly in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
خطمي (khithmi): A scented plant used for washing; considered a perfume in ihram by Abu Hanifa.
طيب (tib): Perfume, any substance with a pleasant scent that is prohibited in ihram.
شعث (sha'ath): Unkemptness, disheveled hair – a desired state for the pilgrim.
تفل (tafil): Having a foul smell from lack of perfume – the ideal state of a pilgrim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the penalty for a muhrim who washes his head with khithmi one time?
A) No penalty, it is permissible
B) Sadaqah according to Abu Hanifa, dam according to Sahibayn
C) Dam according to Abu Hanifa, sadaqah according to Sahibayn
D) Dam by consensus of all Hanafi imams

Key Takeaway
In ihram, any substance with a pleasant scent, including khithmi and henna, is prohibited as perfume, with the penalty varying between dam and sadaqah according to the Hanafi imams.

Prohibition of Hair and Nail Removal in Ihram
States the prohibition of shaving head, cutting hair and nails for a muhrim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibition of removing hair and nails during ihram, derive its evidence from the Quran, and explain the scope of the prohibition including methods of removal.

Core Idea
Shaving the head, cutting any hair, or trimming nails in ihram is prohibited by explicit Quranic text and analogy.

This lesson clarifies the prohibition of grooming actions while in the state of ihram. The text from Kanz al-Daqaiq establishes that not only shaving the head but also cutting any hair or nails is forbidden, based on the Quranic injunction in Surah al-Baqarah (2:196) and the command to complete tafath (fulfillment of ritual cleanliness) after the sacrifice. The prohibition extends to all methods of removal, including burning or lime, and includes trimming the mustache, plucking armpit hair, and shaving pubic hair.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited acts
From the text, list the actions that are prohibited: shaving the head, cutting hair, trimming nails. Note that the text explicitly mentions 'حلق رأسه' (shaving his head), 'قص شعره' (cutting his hair), and 'ظفره' (his nail). Also, from the commentary, trimming the mustache, plucking armpit hair, and shaving pubic hair are included under tafath.
The prohibition covers all types of hair removal, not just the head.
2
Locate the primary proof
Find the direct evidence from Quran: 'وَلا تَحْلِقُوا رُؤُوسَكُمْ حَتَّى يَبْلُغَ الْهَدْيُ مَحِلَّهُ' (al-Baqarah 2:196). This is a clear prohibition (nahy) indicating haram.
The verse explicitly forbids shaving the head until the sacrifice reaches its place.
3
Apply analogy (qiyas) for cutting hair and nails
The text states that cutting is 'in the meaning' of shaving (القص في معنى الحلق). Since both achieve the same result—removal of hair or nail—they share the same ruling via qiyas. The prohibition is thus established by 'dalalat al-nass' (textual indication).
This is a key Hanafi usul principle: the ruling extends to similar actions.
4
Understand the scope of removal methods
The text clarifies that 'shaving' (حلق) means removal (إزالة). Therefore, any method that removes hair—whether by shaving, burning, lime (nurah), or other means—is included. This is because the purpose is to prevent the pilgrim from becoming groomed before the sacrifice.
Even using depilatory creams or laser would be prohibited today under this principle.
5
Note the exception for eye hair
Al-Halabi in his Manasik exempts removal of hair growing in the eye (in the way of the eye). According to the Hanafi school, there is no penalty for this because such hair is a nuisance and necessary to remove for vision or comfort.
This exception is specific; other hair removal requires compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحلق رأسه وقص شعره وظفره لا الاغتسال ودخول الحمام والاستظلال بالبيت والمحمل، وشدّ الهميان
أي واتق أيضاً
وحلق رأسه إلى ظفره قال العيني: فيه التفات من الخطاب إلى الغيبة، لكن في نسخة شرح "ملا مسكين" العبارة هكذا: "وحلق الشعر وقص الشارب والظفر"، وهذه النسخة أنسب بالمذكورات أولاً، ومنع حلق الرأس؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَحْلِقُوا رُؤُوسَكُمْ حَتَّى يَبْلُغَ الْهَدْيُ مَحِلَّهُ ﴾ (البقرة: ١٩٦)، والقص في معنى الحلق، فثبت المنع بدلالة النص؛ وأراد بالحلق الإزالة، فعم ما لو كان بإحراق أو نورة؛ لأنه من إزالة الشعث وقضاء التفث، وقال تعالى: ثُمَّ لْيَقْضُوا تَفَتَهُمْ (الحج: ٢٩) والتفث: الأخذ من الشارب، وتقليم الأظفار، ونتف الإبط، وحلق العانة، والأخذ من الشعر، وقيل: التفث: الوسخ والمراد بالقضاء إزالة التفث، واستثنى الحلبي في "مناسكه" إزالة الشعر النابت في العين، فإنه لا شيء فيه عندنا. (فتح، مستخلص) وقص: أي وليتق أيضاً قطع شعره وقلم ظفره. (عيني)
And shaving his head, cutting his hair and nail – not bathing, entering the bathhouse, seeking shade in the tent, the litter, and fastening the money belt. Also abstain. Shaving his head up to nail: al-Ayni said: There is a shift from second person to third person, but in the version of Sharh Miskin, the expression is: 'And shaving hair, trimming the mustache, and nail.' This version is more appropriate for the previously mentioned things. The prohibition of shaving the head is due to His saying: 'Do not shave your heads until the sacrificial animal reaches its place' (al-Baqarah 2:196). Cutting is like shaving, so prohibition is established by textual indication. He meant by shaving removal, so it includes removal by burning or lime (nurah), because it is removal of shaggy hair and fulfillment of the tafath (ritual cleanliness). And He said: 'Then let them complete their tafath' (al-Hajj 22:29). Tafath means taking from the mustache, trimming nails, plucking armpit hair, shaving pubic hair, and taking from the hair. Some said tafath means dirt, and the meaning of 'fulfill' is removal of dirt. Al-Halabi in his Manasik exempts removal of hair growing in the eye; there is no penalty for it according to us. (Fath, Mustakhlas) And 'Qass': i.e., also abstain from cutting his hair and trimming his nail. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
The text begins the Mubahat al-Ihram section but immediately states the prohibitions that are not part of the permissible list. It explicitly prohibits shaving the head, cutting hair, and trimming nails. The prohibition is derived from the Quranic verse (2:196): 'Do not shave your heads until the sacrificial animal reaches its place.' Cutting hair is analogically included because it falls under the same meaning (dalalat al-nass). The prohibition extends to any method of hair removal, including burning or using lime (nurah), as all constitute removal of the 'shaggy' state (sha'ath) that is part of the pilgrim's required condition. The verse 'then let them complete their tafath' (22:29) is interpreted as the removal of dirt and untidiness, which includes shaving, trimming nails, and other grooming acts prohibited before sacrifice. An exception is noted: hair that grows in the eye (in the way of the eye) does not incur any penalty, as reported from Al-Halabi. The text also notes a variant reading that includes trimming the mustache and cutting nails explicitly. The prohibition is clear: any deliberate removal of hair or nail requires compensation, proportionate to the amount.

Key Terms
حلق (halaq): Shaving the head; prohibited in ihram by Quran.
تفث (tafath): Ritual cleanliness by removing dirt, hair, and nails after completing hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Sadaqah required for three or more nails
C) Dam required even for one nail
D) It is permissible with no penalty

Key Takeaway
In ihram, any deliberate removal of hair or nails is prohibited, based on Quranic text and analogy, and requires compensation.

Bathing and Entering Bathhouses in Ihram
States two permissible acts with their proofs and a disliked element

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bathing in ihram and explain why removing dirt is disliked but not prohibited.

Core Idea
Bathing and entering a bathhouse in ihram is permitted; only actively removing dirt is discouraged.

A common concern for pilgrims is whether they can maintain personal hygiene while in ihram. This lesson clarifies that bathing and even visiting a bathhouse are permissible, based on the Prophet's own practice. However, a crucial distinction is made between merely washing and actively scraping off dirt, which is considered disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The passage addresses two acts: bathing (ightisal) and entering a bathhouse (dukhul al-hammam). First, identify the ruling for each from the text.
Bathing is mubah; entering bathhouse is also mubah.
2
Locate the dalil from the Sunnah
The text cites the Prophet's actions as evidence: he bathed while in ihram (narrated by Muslim) and entered a bathhouse at al-Juhfah.
Prophetic fi'l is proof of permissibility in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the Prophet's actions indicate permissibility. In Hanafi usul, the Prophet's act (fi'l) is evidence of ibahah unless there is a contrary indicator.
The hadith is sahih, so thubut is qat'i; dalalah is zanni because it shows permissibility, not obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with Malik
The passage mentions that Imam Malik requires fidyah if the muhrim rubs his body in the bathhouse. Identify the point of disagreement.
Malik considers rubbing as removing dirt, which is prohibited in ihram.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi pilgrim may rub lightly (though disliked) without penalty, while a Maliki must offer fidyah if he rubs.
This shows how different usul principles lead to different rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاغتسال: لأنه علي اغتسل وهو محرم، رواه مسلم (عيني)
ودخول الحمام لأنه علي دخل الحمام بالجحفة، وقال: "ما يعبأ الله بأوساخنا شيئاً"، والمراد مجرد دخول الحمام والاغتسال بالماء الحار، وأما إزالة الوسخ فمكروهة، وعند مالك: إن دخل الحمام وتدلك افتدى رمز الحقائق: ١٥٤/١]
Bathing: because he (the Prophet) bathed while in ihram, narrated by Muslim. (Ayni) And entering the bathhouse: because he entered the bathhouse at al-Juhfah and said: 'Allah does not regard our dirt as anything.' The intent is merely entering the bathhouse and bathing with hot water. As for removing dirt, it is disliked. According to Malik: if he enters the bathhouse and rubs (his body), he must compensate. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/154)
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage addresses two permissible acts for a muhrim: bathing (ghusl) and entering a bathhouse (hammam). Both are allowed based on the Prophet's practice. The proof for bathing is that the Prophet bathed while in ihram (Muslim). For entering a bathhouse, the Prophet entered one at al-Juhfah and remarked that Allah does not regard the dirt of pilgrims as anything, implying that cleanliness is not contrary to the spirit of ihram. However, a distinction is made: merely entering and bathing with hot water is permissible, but actively removing dirt (e.g., scrubbing) is disliked (makruh). Imam Malik holds a stricter view: if a muhrim enters a bathhouse and rubs his body (thereby removing dirt), he must offer a compensation (fidyah). The Hanafi position is more lenient, allowing bathing and bathhouse entry as long as one does not deliberately remove dirt. This ruling highlights that the pilgrim may maintain basic hygiene without violating the prohibitions of ihram.

Key Terms
اغتسال (ightisal): Bathing; permissible in ihram based on Prophetic example.
حمام (hammam): Bathhouse; entering is permissible in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible, no penalty
B) Disliked, but no fidyah required
C) Haram, requires dam
D) Permissible only if not rubbing

Key Takeaway
Bathing and entering a bathhouse are permissible for a muhrim; only actively removing dirt is disliked and does not require compensation.

Seeking Shade in Ihram: The Hanafi Position
States the Hanafi rule on shade in ihram with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on seeking shade in ihram, identify the conditions for its permissibility, and explain the Hanafi rebuttal to the Maliki/Hanbali objection.

Core Idea
Seeking shade is permissible unless it directly touches your head or face.

A muhrim (one in ihram) faces the sun and heat during Hajj; can he seek shade? The answer involves a classic dispute between the Hanafi school and the schools of Malik and Ahmad. The Hanafi school permits it, based on the practice of the Prophet ﷺ and the Caliphs, with the key condition that the shade does not directly cover the head or face. Let's unpack the evidence and the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its condition
Identify the core ruling: permissibility (mubah) with the condition that the shade does not touch the head or face. Understand the expanded definition of 'bayt' to include any shade-providing structure.
The condition is critical: if it touches, it becomes makruh.
2
Identify the opposing school and its proof
Malik and Ahmad hold that seeking shade under a tent or similar is disliked. Their evidence: the report from Umar telling a man to 'bare yourself for the One for Whom you have entered ihram.'
Understand the basis of their ikhtilaf.
3
Follow the Hanafi evidence (dalil)
The Hanafi school relies on the hadith of Umm Hushayn: during the Farewell Pilgrimage, Bilal shaded the Prophet with a cloth. Also, the practice of Umar and Uthman (who sought shade) is cited.
Prophetic action and Caliphal consensus outweigh an isolated statement.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: the Prophet's explicit practice and the actions of the Rightly-Guided Caliphs take precedence over a single report from Umar, which may have been context-specific.
This is a classic tarjih between contradictory evidences.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: Hanafis allow using tents, umbrellas, and litters as long as not covering head/face; Malikis and Hanbalis consider it disliked or prohibited.
This affects millions of pilgrims each year.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاستظلال بالبيت وهو في الأصل الخيمة من الصوف أو الشعر، ثم أطلق على المسقف، سمي به؛ لأنه يبات فيه، وفي معناه نطع أو ثوب مرفوع على عود بحيث يمكن الاستظلال به، وكذا الفسطاط والمحمل إن لم يصب رأسه أو وجهه، فإن أصاب أحدهما كره وقال مالك: يكره أن يستظل بالفسطاط وما أشبهه؛ لما أن روي عمر ابن ما أمر رجلا قد رفع ثوبا على عود يستتر من الشمس فقال له: "أضح لمن أحرمت له" أي أبرز، وبه قال أحمد، ولنا: حديث أم الحصين قالت: "حججت مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حجة الوداع فرأيت أسامة وبلالاً أحدهما آخذ بخطام ناقة النبي والآخر رافع ثوبه يستره من الحر حتى رمى جمرة العقبة"، رواه مسلم وغيره. [رمز الحقائق: ١٥٤/١] وعمر الله كان يلقي على شجرة ثوباً ويستظل به، وعثمان له نصب له فسطاط. (فتح) والمحمل: بفتح الميم الأولى وكسر الثانية أو على العكس الهودج الكبير الحجازي، وهذا إذا لم يمس رأسه ولا وجهه وإلا كره (ط) (مسكين
And seeking shade in the tent (bayt), which originally is a tent made of wool or hair, then it was applied to any roofed structure, named so because one spends the night in it. Included in its meaning is a rug or a cloth raised on a stick such that one can seek shade with it. Similarly, the large tent (fustat) and the litter (mahmil), provided it does not touch his head or face. If it touches either, it becomes disliked. Malik said: It is disliked to seek shade under a fustat and the like, because it is narrated that 'Umar ibn al-Khattab saw a man who had raised a cloth on a stick to shelter from the sun, so he said to him: 'Bare yourself for the One for whom you have entered ihram,' i.e., expose yourself. Ahmad also held this view. Our evidence is the hadith of Umm Hushayn who said: 'I performed Hajj with the Messenger of Allah during the Farewell Pilgrimage, and I saw Usamah and Bilal: one was holding the nose-string of the Prophet's she-camel, and the other was raising his cloth to shade him from the heat until he stoned the Jamrat al-Aqabah.' Narrated by Muslim and others. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/154) And 'Umar himself used to throw a cloth over a tree and seek shade under it, and 'Uthman had a fustat set up for him. (Fath) And al-mahmil: with fatha on the first meem and kasra on the second, or vice versa, is the large Hijazi litter. This ruling applies if it does not touch his head or face; otherwise, it is disliked. (T, Miskin)
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of seeking shade during ihram, a topic of dispute between the Hanafi school and the schools of Malik and Ahmad. The text defines 'bayt' (tent) as originally a wool or hair tent, later extended to any structure that provides shade. Seeking shade under a cloth raised on a stick, a large tent (fustat), or a litter (mahmil) is permissible on the condition that the shade does not touch the muhrim's head or face. If it does touch, it becomes disliked (makruh). The Hanafi evidence is the hadith of Umm Hushayn, who saw Bilal raising his cloth to shade the Prophet during the Farewell Pilgrimage until he stoned the Jamrah. This clearly shows the Prophet allowed being shaded. Additionally, the practice of Umar and Uthman (who used to seek shade under a cloth or tent) is cited. The opposing view of Malik and Ahmad is based on the narration that Umar told a man to 'expose yourself to the One for Whom you have entered ihram,' implying that shading oneself contradicts the spirit of ihram. The Hanafi response is that the Prophet's action and the practice of the Rightly-Guided Caliphs take precedence over a single statement attributed to Umar, which may have been situational. Thus, seeking shade is permissible as long as it does not directly cover the head or face.

Key Terms
استظلال (istizlal): Seeking shade; permissible in ihram if the shade does not directly cover the head/face.
بيت (bayt): Tent or any structure providing shade.
محمل (mahmil): A litter (howdaj) used for travel; seeking shade under it is permissible if it does not touch head/face.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A muhrim sits under a tree that provides shade without touching his head. According to the Hanafi school, this is:
A) Permissible, no penalty
B) Disliked, but no penalty
C) Haram, requires dam
D) Permissible only if not intentional

Key Takeaway
Seeking shade in ihram is permissible (mubah) in the Hanafi school as long as the shade does not touch the muhrim's head or face.

Permissibility of Fastening a Money Belt and Other Acts in Ihram
Ruling on several permissible acts in ihram with conditions and a cross-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on permissible acts in ihram (money belt, belt, sword, ring, kohl, medical treatments) and identify the conditions that trigger compensation for scented kohl and accidental hair/louse removal.

Core Idea
Practical needs like money belts and medical treatments are permissible unless they involve perfume or direct hair removal.

Having covered the prohibited and disliked acts in ihram, the Hanafi school now addresses a set of permissible acts that a muhrim may need. These include fastening a money belt, wearing a belt or sword, applying unscented kohl, and seeking medical treatments. The passage also lays down graded compensation for scented kohl and accidental hair loss, and briefly addresses a Maliki objection to the money belt.

Guided Path
1
List the permissible acts
Identify each act mentioned: fastening a money belt (himyan), wearing a belt (mintaqah), sword, weapon, ring, applying kohl, phlebotomy, cupping, tooth extraction, bone setting, scratching head/body.
These are all mubah (permissible) in the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the condition for scented kohl
Note the gradation: once or twice requires sadaqah (charity); many times requires a dam (blood sacrifice). Determine what counts as 'many' – in Hanafi fiqh, three or more applications constitute frequent use.
Compensation is only for scented (perfumed) kohl; unscented is fully mubah.
3
Examine medical and grooming acts
Phlebotomy, cupping, tooth extraction, bone setting are permissible without compensation. Scratching the head/body is allowed gently; if a hair or louse falls, a small sadaqah for one, a handful of food for three.
The caution 'if he fears a hair falling' indicates that intentional removal is penalized, but incidental loss is treated lightly.
4
Understand the Maliki dispute
Imam Malik dislikes the money belt if it contains someone else's spending money and requires fidyah. The Hanafi school responds by citing the authentic hadith of Aisha (ra) which permits it, and dismisses the Maliki proof as weak.
This is an example of istidlal (proof-based reasoning) and tarjih (preference) within fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشدّ الهميان [بكسر الهاء كيس الدراهم يشد على الحقو. (ط،ع)] وكذا منطقة وسيف وسلاح وتختم واكتحال بغير مطيب، فلو اكتحل بمطيب مرة أو مرتين فعليه صدقة، ولو كثيرا فعليه دم، وكذا فصد وحجامة وقلع ضرس وجبر كسر وحك الرأس والبدن، لكن برفق إن خاف سقوط شعرة أو قملة، ففي الواحدة يتصدق بشيء، وفي الثلاث كفّ من طعام، وقوله: "في وسطه"، المراد بالوسط الخصر، وهو من البدن فوق الوركين وقال مالك: يكره شد الهميان إن كان فيه نفقة غيره، وإن شد افتدى؛ لضعف الحديث, وحديث عائشة في الصحيح. (فتح, رمز الحقائق)
And fastening the himyan: with kasra on the ha, a purse of dirhams tied at the waist. (T, Ayni) Similarly, a belt (mintaqah), a sword, a weapon, a ring, and applying kohl without perfume. If he applies kohl with perfume once or twice, sadaqah is due; if many times, a dam is due. Similarly, phlebotomy (fasd), cupping (hijamah), tooth extraction, bone setting, and scratching the head and body, but gently if he fears a hair or louse falling. For one, he gives sadaqah of something; for three, a handful of food. And his saying: 'in his waist', meaning the waist (khasir), which is above the hips. Malik said: It is disliked to fasten the himyan if it contains someone else's spending (nafaqah), and if he does so, he must compensate (fidyah) due to the weakness of the hadith; the hadith of Aisha is sahih. (Fath, Ramz al-Haqa'iq)
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This final passage of the Mubahat al-Ihram section covers a range of permissible acts: fastening a money belt (himyan), wearing a belt (mintaqah), sword, weapon, ring, and applying kohl (without perfume). These are all allowed as they do not constitute the prohibited covering or scent. However, if scented kohl is used, compensation varies: once or twice requires sadaqah; frequent use requires a dam. Other medical acts like phlebotomy, cupping, tooth extraction, bone setting, and gentle scratching of the head/body are permissible but with caution: if a hair or louse falls, a small sadaqah is given for one, a handful of food for three. The text also clarifies that 'waist' (wast) refers to the middle part above the hips. A dispute with Malik is mentioned: Malik dislikes fastening a money belt if it contains someone else's spending money, and requires fidyah if done, citing a weak hadith. The Hanafi school relies on the authentic hadith from Aisha (which permits it) and thus considers it permissible without penalty. Thus, the Hanafi position is more lenient, allowing these practical necessities as long as they do not involve perfume or direct covering of the head/face.

Key Terms
هميان (himyan): A money belt worn at the waist; permissible in ihram.
اكتحال (iktihal): Applying kohl to the eyes; permissible only if without perfume.
فصد (fasd): Phlebotomy (bloodletting); permissible in ihram.
مطيب (mutayyib): Perfumed/scented; referring to kohl containing perfume.
صدقة (sadaqah): Charity; compensation for minor infractions in ihram.
دم (dam): Blood sacrifice; compensation for major infractions in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No compensation, it is permissible
B) Sadaqah for each application
C) One dam for frequent use
D) Nothing unless more than ten times

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a range of practical acts in ihram, with compensation only when perfume or hair removal is involved.

Permissible and Prohibited Acts During Ihram (Hanafi View)
Ruling with proof on permissible acts during ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on bathing, shading, shaving, and wearing a money belt during ihram, and explain the proof for shading permission.

Core Idea
Bathing and shading are permitted during ihram; shaving hair is prohibited until sacrifice.

This passage defines several permissible and prohibited acts during the state of ihram. While shaving the head is clearly prohibited by the Quran, other actions like bathing, entering a bathhouse, and using shade are permitted by the Prophet's practice. The commentary also clarifies the ruling on the money belt (himyan) and when certain actions require penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify Prohibited Acts
From the opening sentence, list acts that are forbidden: shaving the head, cutting hair, trimming nails. Note that these are prohibited by the Quran and analogy.
The term 'tafath' (fulfilling needs) is interpreted as removing hair and dirt after the rituals.
2
Distinguish Permitted Acts
Bathing, entering a bathhouse, and shading under a tent or structure are explicitly stated as not prohibited. The Prophet bathed and entered a bathhouse in ihram, establishing permission.
Removing dirt by rubbing is disliked (makruh), but simple bathing is mubah.
3
Analyze the Shading Dispute
Hanafis permit shading as long as it does not touch the head or face. Proof: Hadith of Umm al-Husayn (Bilal shaded the Prophet). Malik and Ahmad consider it disliked, citing Umar's athar. The Hanafi response is that the hadith is stronger and practices of Umar and Uthman support it.
The dispute is a legitimate ikhtilaf; Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
4
Examine the Money Belt (Himyan) and Similar Items
The himyan (money belt), belt, sword, ring, and kohl without perfume are permitted. Malik dislikes himyan if it contains another's money; Hanafis permit based on Ibn Abbas's saying and lack of prohibition.
Kohl with perfume requires a penalty (sadaqah or dam).
5
Understand Other Actions and Penalties
Actions like cupping, tooth extraction, scratching that cause hair loss require light sadaqah. The 'in his middle' refers to the waist area.
Penalties scale based on the number of hairs or actions.

Permissible acts of ihram: and shaving his head, cutting his hair and nail – not bathing, entering the bathhouse, and shading with the house; and also the howdah and tying the money belt. And shaving his head up to his nail: al-Ayni said: there is a shift from address to third person, but in a copy of Sharh Mulla Miskin the phrase is: 'and shaving the hair, trimming the mustache, and the nail', and this copy is more appropriate to what was mentioned first. And prohibition of shaving the head due to His statement: 'And do not shave your heads until the sacrificial animal reaches its place of sacrifice' (2:196), and cutting is in the meaning of shaving, so the prohibition is established by implication of the text; and by 'shaving' he meant removal, so it includes when done by burning or lime, because it is part of removing dishevelment and fulfilling the 'tafath', and He said: 'Then let them complete their rites and fulfill their vows and perform Tawaf around the Ancient House' (22:29) and 'tafath' is: taking from the mustache, trimming the nails, plucking the armpit, shaving the pubic area, and taking from the hair; and it is said: 'tafath' is dirt, and the intended meaning of 'qada' (fulfilling) is removing the tafath. And al-Halabi in his 'Manasik' exempted removing hair growing in the eye, for there is no penalty in it according to us. (Fath, Mustakhlas) 'And cut': i.e., and also let him avoid cutting his hair and trimming his nail. (Ayni) '(Not) bathing': because he (peace be upon him) bathed while in ihram, narrated by Muslim (Ayni) 'And entering the bathhouse': because he (peace be upon him) entered the bathhouse at al-Juhfa, and said: 'Allah does not regard our dirt as anything', and the intended meaning is merely entering the bathhouse and bathing with hot water; as for removing dirt, it is disliked. And according to Malik: if he enters the bathhouse and rubs himself, he must offer a fidyah. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/154] 'And shading with the house': originally it is a tent of wool or hair, then it was applied to a roofed structure, so named because one spends the night in it; and in its meaning is a leather mat or a cloth raised on a stick such that shading can be achieved under it; likewise the pavilion and the howdah if it does not touch his head or face; if it touches either, it is disliked. And Malik said: it is disliked to shade under the pavilion and the like, because of what was narrated from Umar ibn al-Khattab that he commanded a man who had raised a cloth on a stick to screen himself from the sun, saying to him: 'Expose yourself to the one for whom you have entered ihram' – meaning appear. And Ahmad agreed with this. And our proof is the hadith of Umm al-Husayn who said: 'I performed Hajj with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) during the Farewell Pilgrimage and I saw Usama and Bilal, one holding the nose-ring of the Prophet's she-camel and the other raising his garment to shelter him from the heat until he stoned the Jamrat al-Aqaba', narrated by Muslim and others. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/154] And Umar ibn al-Khattab used to place a garment over a tree and shade under it, and Uthman had a pavilion set up for him. (Fath) 'And the howdah': with a fatha on the first meem and a kasra on the second, or the reverse, is the large Hijazi camel litter; this is if it does not touch his head or face; otherwise it is disliked. (T, Miskin) 'And tying the himyan': with a kasra on the ha, it is a bag of dirhams tied around the waist. (T, A) and likewise a belt, sword, weapon, wearing a ring, and applying kohl without perfume. If he applies kohl with perfume once or twice, he must give sadaqah; if much, he must offer a dam. Likewise phlebotomy, cupping, tooth extraction, bone-setting, and scratching the head and body, but gently if he fears falling of a hair or a louse; for one, he gives a little sadaqah; for three, a handful of food. And his saying: 'in his middle': the intended meaning of 'middle' is the waist, which is from the body above the hips. And Malik said: it is disliked to tie the himyan if it contains the money of another; if he ties it, he must offer a fidyah; because Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) said: 'Secure your expenses to you as you wish' when asked about it; and because there is no need for it, so it is not permissible. In contrast to: the narrow place, because what was narrated about in his middle.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is from the section on permissible acts during ihram (mubahat al-ihram). It begins by listing actions that are prohibited (shaving the head, cutting hair and nails) and then clarifies that bathing, entering a bathhouse, and shading with a tent are not prohibited. The commentary includes proofs: for the prohibition of shaving, the Quranic verse (2:196) and the concept of 'tafath' from 22:29. The Hanafi position is that shaving the head includes any method of hair removal (burning, lime). Al-Halabi exempted hair growing in the eye. For bathing, the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) bathing while in ihram is cited. Entering the bathhouse is permitted based on the Prophet's action at al-Juhfa, though removing dirt is disliked. The shading dispute: The Hanafi school permits shading under a tent, pavilion, or howdah as long as it does not touch the head or face, based on the hadith of Umm al-Husayn where Bilal sheltered the Prophet with a garment. Malik and Ahmad consider it disliked, citing an athar of Umar instructing a man to expose himself to the sun. The Hanafi response is that the Prophetic hadith takes precedence. The ruling on the howdah: it is permissible as long as it does not touch the head or face, otherwise it becomes disliked. The himyan (money belt) and similar items (belt, sword, ring, kohl without perfume) are permitted, but if kohl with perfume is used, sadaqah or dam may be required. Malik disliked tying the himyan if it contains another's money, but the Hanafi position permits it without restriction, based on the practice of Ibn Abbas and the lack of evidence for prohibition. The passage also covers rulings on phlebotomy, cupping, etc., where minimal actions require sadaqah and more require a handful of food. The commentary ends with a clarification of 'in his middle' referring to the waist area.

Key Terms
الهميان (al-himyān): A money bag tied around the waist (the himyan) that is permitted during ihram according to Hanafis.
التفث (al-tafath): The state of dishevelment and dirt that the pilgrim is required to remove after completing rituals; includes trimming nails, plucking armpit hair, etc.
المحمل (al-mahmil): A large camel litter (howdah) used by pilgrims; its use is permitted during ihram if it does not touch the head or face.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Permissible as long as it does not touch the head
C) Disliked under all circumstances
D) Permissible only if it is attached to a tent

Key Takeaway
Shading and bathing are permitted during ihram; shaving hair is prohibited until after sacrifice.

Hajj — Pilgrimage
